href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="32" height="16" class="noviewer" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/64px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:United_Kingdom" title="Portal:United Kingdom">United Kingdom&#32;portal</a>  <div class="hlist" style="text-align:center;font-weight:normal;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_political_science#Politics_by_region" title="Outline of political science">Other countries</a></li>\n<li><a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Atlas_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="extiw" title="commons:Atlas of the United Kingdom">Atlas</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:Politics of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:Politics of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Human rights in the United Kingdom</b> are set out in <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>, with its strongest roots being in the <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">English</a> <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> <a href="/wiki/Claim_of_Right_Act_1689" title="Claim of Right Act 1689">Claim of Right Act 1689</a>, as well as legislation of European institutions: the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, it has been alleged that the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a> has also had a history of  both <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> discrimination,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and, in recent history, occasional violations of basic human rights, particularly in times of <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a> crises, or regarding the rights of <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration to the United Kingdom">migrants</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> the unemployed,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and the disabled.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In recent years, however, British human rights legislation has been criticised by some for what they perceive as excessive attention to the human rights of offenders at the expense of those of victims; high-profile cases, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Learco_Chindamo" class="mw-redirect" title="Learco Chindamo">Learco Chindamo</a><sup id="cite_ref-dc_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dc-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/2006_Afghan_hijackers_case" title="2006 Afghan hijackers case">2006 Afghan hijackers</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-2af_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2af-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> have attracted controversy, sparking calls<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2016)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> for the review of the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a> and other legislation. <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Second_Cameron_ministry" title="Second Cameron ministry">second ministry</a> announced plans to replace the Human rights Act with a "<a href="/wiki/Proposed_British_Bill_of_Rights" title="Proposed British Bill of Rights">British Bill of Rights</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Cases_involving_the_UK_before_the_European_Court_of_Human_Rights"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Cases involving the UK before the class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Footnotes"><span links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_approach_to_rights_and_liberties">The approach to rights and liberties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: The approach to rights and liberties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;">The Bill of Rights</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg" class="image"><img alt="English Bill of Rights of 1689.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg/220px-English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg" width="220" height="444" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/English_Bill_of_Rights_of_1689.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="304" data-file-height="614" /></a><div>The <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights</a> (1689)</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Ratification" title="Ratification">Ratified</a></th><td>\n16 December 1689</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Location</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Archives" title="Parliamentary Archives">Parliamentary Archives</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Author(s)</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Purpose</th><td>\nAssert certain rights.</td></tr></tbody></table> \n<p><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, issued in 1215, explicitly protected certain rights of the King\'s <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">subjects</a> and restricted the power of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">King of England</a>. It implicitly supported what became the writ of <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a>, safeguarding individual freedom against <a href="/wiki/False_imprisonment" title="False imprisonment">unlawful imprisonment</a> with right to <a href="/wiki/Appeal" title="Appeal">appeal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort%27s_Parliament" title="Simon de Montfort&#39;s Parliament">first representative English parliament</a> in 1265, the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England#The_emergence_of_parliament_as_an_institution" title="Parliament of England">petitioning</a> in the 13th century is some of the earliest evidence of parliament being used as a forum to address the general grievances of ordinary people.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The idea of freely debating rights to political representation took form during the <a href="/wiki/Putney_Debates" title="Putney Debates">Putney Debates</a> of 1647. Beginning in the late 17th century, philosophers began to think of rights not as privileges to be granted by the government or the law, but as a fundamental part of what it means to be a person.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> (1632\xe2\x80\x931704), one of the most influential of <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> thinkers, argued that property - which to him included life, liberty and possessions - was an unalienable human right, that is, one which cannot be taken away. He articulated that every person is created equal and free but, in return for the advantages of living in an organised society, a person may need to give up some of this freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a>, enacted in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> (which at the time included Wales), reinforced the <a href="/wiki/Petition_of_Right" title="Petition of Right">Petition of Right</a> (1628) and the <a href="/wiki/Habeas_Corpus_Act_1679" title="Habeas Corpus Act 1679">Habaes Corpus Act</a> (1679) by codifying certain rights and liberties. It established the prohibition against <a href="/wiki/Cruel_and_unusual_punishment" title="Cruel and unusual punishment">cruel and unusual punishment</a> and limited the power of the monarch.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A separate but similar document, the <a href="/wiki/Claim_of_Right_Act_1689" title="Claim of Right Act 1689">Claim of Right Act 1689</a>, was enacted in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a>; neither applies in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SN00293_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SN00293-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> started to become a principle in the way the country is governed.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_domestic_tradition">The domestic tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The domestic tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Civil liberties in the United Kingdom">Civil liberties in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>In the place of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty" title="Popular sovereignty">popular sovereignty</a> found in other democracies, the United Kingdom evolved a concept of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliamentary sovereignty in the United Kingdom">parliamentary sovereignty</a> under which the liberties of the individual are implications drawn from two principles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._101_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._101-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> First, the "residual liberty" to act as one wishes so long as the activity is not restricted by the law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPannickLester2004para._1.02_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPannickLester2004para._1.02-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Second, public authorities and <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">The Crown</a> can do only that which is authorised by law, and in particular may not interfere with individuals\' liberties without <a href="/wiki/Statutory_authority" title="Statutory authority">statutory authority</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._101_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._101-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In this perspective, the vision of individual rights is perceived more in terms of an "undifferentiated mass of liberty" rather than a bundle of separate positive liberties and freedoms which define the relationship between citizen and state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200270_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200270-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> As Parliament is sovereign, liberties are freely alterable under the supervision of citizens\' elected representatives who ensure that any encroachment is controlled and authorised for democratic purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200270_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200270-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moves_toward_change">Moves toward change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Moves toward change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the 20th century, a belief arose that some extra protection of human rights above and beyond parliamentary scrutiny was necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200956_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200956-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Doubts grew about the capacity of parliamentary controls, partly because of scepticism about Parliament\'s will to control the growing executive and the control by political parties of their <a href="/wiki/Member_of_parliament" title="Member of parliament">MPs</a> which allowed weak governments to avoid effective challenges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200271_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200271-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> This scepticism went hand in hand with criticisms of the United Kingdom\'s political system and whether it is sufficiently representative of the range of opinion in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200271_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200271-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The doubts intensified with the experiences with <a href="/wiki/List_of_terrorist_incidents_in_Great_Britain" title="List of terrorist incidents in Great Britain">terrorism</a> in the 1970s and accession to the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Community</a> in 1973 where Britain was exposed to other legal systems which did not share the same concept of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty" title="Parliamentary sovereignty">parliamentary sovereignty</a> and which gave stronger protection to human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In particular, European entry led to the notion that the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a> could be subject to the decisions of a higher legal order in the form of the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> This was highlighted in the <a href="/wiki/Factortame_litigation" class="mw-redirect" title="Factortame litigation">Factortame litigation</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Judicial_functions_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Judicial functions of the House of Lords">House of Lords</a> was required to \'disapply\' provisions of an <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Parliament_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Acts of Parliament in the United Kingdom">Act of Parliament</a> which were contrary to <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Community law</a>, effectively ruling them invalid.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In addition, increasingly influenced by international human rights law, comparative constitutional law and European law, English courts became more sympathetic towards the concept of popular sovereignty and fundamental rights and liberties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._101_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._101-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation">Legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Convention_on_Human_Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: European Convention on Human Rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="UK_role">UK role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: UK role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The UK played an important role in the drafting of the Convention,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.59_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.59-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> with figures such as <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Lehman_Goodhart" title="Arthur Lehman Goodhart">Arthur Goodhart</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_(UK_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Foster (UK politician)">John Foster</a> and the UK-based <a href="/wiki/Hersch_Lauterpacht" title="Hersch Lauterpacht">Hersch Lauterpacht</a> providing the impetus for the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> in 1949 as a means of guarding against the rise of new <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship" title="Dictatorship">dictatorships</a> and to provide the citizens of <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a>-occupied countries with a beacon of hope.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The initiative in producing a legally binding human rights agreement had already been taken by the International Council of the <a href="/wiki/European_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="European Movement">European Movement</a>, an organisation whose cause had been championed by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>, and whose international juridical section (counting Lauterpacht and <a href="/wiki/David_Maxwell_Fyfe,_1st_Earl_of_Kilmuir" title="David Maxwell Fyfe, 1st Earl of Kilmuir">Maxwell Fyfe</a> amongst its members) had produced a draft convention.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Chaired by Maxwell Fyfe and the former <a href="/wiki/French_Resistance" title="French Resistance">French Resistance</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Henri_Teitgen" title="Pierre-Henri Teitgen">Pierre-Henri Teitgen</a>, the Legal Committee of the Council of Europe\'s Consultative Assembly proposed that the Council\'s Committee of Ministers draw up a convention which would take in and ensure the effective enjoyment of the rights proclaimed in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> of 10 December 1948, as well as establishing a <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court and Commission of Human Rights</a>. The Committee agreed and the text of what was to become the European Convention of Human Rights was in the main drafted by Sir Oscar Dowson, a retired senior legal adviser to the <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.47_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.47-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ratification">Ratification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ratification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There was reluctant support for the Convention back in the UK where <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Attlee</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Attlee_ministry" title="Attlee ministry">Labour government</a> were in power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200272_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200272-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Jowitt,_1st_Earl_Jowitt" title="William Jowitt, 1st Earl Jowitt">Jowitt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">Colonial Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jim_Griffiths" title="Jim Griffiths">Griffiths</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Sir_Stafford_Cripps" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Stafford Cripps">Sir Stafford Cripps</a> disapproved of ratification on the basis of the loss of sovereignty that would result.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000444-445_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000444-445-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Jowitt also saw a threat to the domestic system of common law and the risk in allowing judgments to be made by an unknown foreign court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000445_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000445-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> He nevertheless believed that ratification was necessary from a political point of view as a refusal would be, in his view, difficult to justify at home and abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000446_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000446-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Political pressure aside, the overall government view was that the Convention was only aimed at preventing a totalitarian takeover and not human rights issues within a functioning democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000447_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000447-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In essence, it was thought that Britain had little to lose from ratification but rejection could risk some loss of face in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000447_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000447-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The Convention had more support among Conservative party politicians, in particular Winston Churchill who believed that it could help unify Europe on the basis of the rule of law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdanor200957-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The UK became the first state to deposit its instrument of ratification of the Convention on 8 March 1951,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007141_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007141-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> with the Convention taking effect on 3 September 1953 after the tenth ratification was deposited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarston1993796_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarston1993796-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.47_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.47-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> No legislation was introduced and no steps were taken to give effect to the Convention\'s rights in domestic law until the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.05_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.05-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_of_petition">Right of petition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Right of petition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Two aspects of the Convention gave the United Kingdom\'s representatives to the Council of Europe cause for concern: the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> and the right of individual petition to the Court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000448_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000448-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> It was thought that the possibility to submit complaints was open to abuse and could be used for political purposes, notably by members of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain" title="Communist Party of Great Britain">Communist Party</a>, rather than for genuine grievances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000448_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000448-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In addition, it was feared that the consequences of accepting individual petition would be to cause instability in the <a href="/wiki/Crown_colony" title="Crown colony">British colonies</a> as Britain\'s authority could be undermined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000448-449_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000448-449-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Britain\'s attempts to exclude the provisions relating to these two issues were unsuccessful but it managed to ensure that they were optional.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000451_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000451-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Third_Churchill_ministry" title="Third Churchill ministry">Conservative government</a> <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">elected in 1951</a> ratified the First Protocol to the ECHR in 1953 relating to the rights to property, education and free elections,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.18_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.18-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> but resisted the right of petition on the grounds that the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> would come under scrutiny by an <a href="/wiki/International_court" title="International court">international court</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Labour\'s <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1964" title="United Kingdom general election, 1964">election in 1964</a> led to reconsideration of the issue and this time the only senior dissenting voice was that of the <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Soskice,_Baron_Stow_Hill" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Soskice, Baron Stow Hill">Frank Soskice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000452_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000452-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Although willing to accept the right of petition, he objected to the compulsory jurisdiction of the Court on the basis that it would deprive the United Kingdom of a degree of flexibility in dealing with petitions and could lead to political embarrassment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000452-453_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000452-453-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, by 1965 a majority of government ministers believed that the Court would not pose a significantly greater threat to <a href="/wiki/Westphalian_sovereignty" title="Westphalian sovereignty">national sovereignty</a> or to political survival than the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission_of_Human_Rights" title="European Commission of Human Rights">Commission of Human Rights</a> to which the United Kingdom was subject.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000453_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000453-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Further pressure for acceptance came from the British judge and President of the Court, <a href="/wiki/Arnold_McNair,_1st_Baron_McNair" title="Arnold McNair, 1st Baron McNair">Arnold McNair, 1st Baron McNair</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_General_of_the_Council_of_Europe" title="Secretary General of the Council of Europe">Secretary General of the Council of Europe</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Smithers" title="Peter Smithers">Peter Smithers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terence_Higgins,_Baron_Higgins" title="Terence Higgins, Baron Higgins">Terence Higgins MP</a> and various non-governmental organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000453-454_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000453-454-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELester1998239_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELester1998239-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As had been the case for ratification in 1950, the Labour government concluded in 1965 that there was little to be lost from giving in to the pressure to recognise the Court and the right of petition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWicks2000454_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWicks2000454-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In December 1965, Wilson informed the <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a> that the right of petition would be recognised for an initial period of three years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELester1998251_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELester1998251-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> At the time the government were concerned that the <a href="/wiki/Burmah_Oil_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Burmah Oil Company">Burmah Oil Company</a> would take advantage of the new right to contest the legality of the <a href="/wiki/War_Damage_Act_1965" title="War Damage Act 1965">War Damage Act 1965</a> depriving the company of the right to compensation for damage caused during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> as recognised by <a href="/wiki/Burmah_Oil_Co._v_Lord_Advocate" class="mw-redirect" title="Burmah Oil Co. v Lord Advocate">a House of Lords\' decision</a>, the acceptance was timed to fall outside the six-month <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_limitations" title="Statute of limitations">limitation period</a> for challenges to the Act and the United Kingdom\'s acceptance also explicitly stated that it applied only to claims arising after its effective date.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELester1998252_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELester1998252-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.48_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.48-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The declarations of acceptance were deposited by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boothby,_Baron_Boothby" title="Robert Boothby, Baron Boothby">Robert Boothby MP</a> with the Secretary General of the Council of Europe on 14 January 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELester1998253_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELester1998253-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_for_incorporation">Campaign for incorporation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Campaign for incorporation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The first public call for the <a href="/wiki/Incorporation_of_international_law" title="Incorporation of international law">incorporation</a> of the Convention into national law was made in 1968 by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Lester,_Baron_Lester_of_Herne_Hill" title="Anthony Lester, Baron Lester of Herne Hill">Anthony Lester</a> who published a pamphlet entitled <i>Democracy and Individual Rights</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.19_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.19-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Then in 1974 <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Scarman,_Baron_Scarman" title="Leslie Scarman, Baron Scarman">Lord Scarman</a> called for an <a href="/wiki/Entrenched_clause" title="Entrenched clause">entrenched</a> instrument to challenge the sovereignty of Parliament and protect basic human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.19_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.19-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.50_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.50-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The following year a Charter of Human Rights was unveiled by the Labour Party <a href="/wiki/National_Executive_Committee" title="National Executive Committee">National Executive Committee</a>, although this was regarded as insufficient by certain Conservative politicians including <a href="/wiki/Leon_Brittan" title="Leon Brittan">Leon Brittan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a> who saw an entrenched Bill of Rights as more effective in preventing abuses by the executive of individual rights <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.20_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.20-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In 1976, a draft Bill of Rights was moved in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Wade,_Baron_Wade" title="Donald Wade, Baron Wade">Lord Wade</a> and in 1978 a House of Lords <a href="/wiki/Select_Committee_(Westminster_System)" class="mw-redirect" title="Select Committee (Westminster System)">Select Committee</a> published a report recommending incorporation which was debated in the Lords leading to an amendment being moved which was requesting that the government introduce legislation on the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.21_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.21-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200278_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200278-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Lord Wade succeeded in securing the Lords\' approval for a draft <a href="/wiki/Bill_(proposed_law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bill (proposed law)">bill</a> but it did not make any progress in the Commons where <a href="/wiki/Alan_Beith" title="Alan Beith">Alan Beith</a>\'s unsuccessful attempt to secure a <a href="/wiki/Second_reading" class="mw-redirect" title="Second reading">second reading</a> was poorly attended.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> There was concern at the time about judges deciding cases involving human rights which could lead them from their traditionally impartial role to political issues <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.21_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.21-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 1986 <a href="/wiki/Derek_Walker-Smith,_Baron_Broxbourne" title="Derek Walker-Smith, Baron Broxbourne">Lord Broxbourne</a> secured the Lords\' approval for his incorporation bill, but was unsuccessful in the Commons, even though a second reading had been obtained.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200278_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200278-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.55_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.55-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gardner_(British_politician)" title="Edward Gardner (British politician)">Edward Gardner</a>\'s 1989 bill to incorporate the Convention also failed on its second reading.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007143_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007143-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.55_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.55-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> At the time, official Conservative party policy was in favour of maintaining the existing constitutional arrangements which it saw as ensuring a high standard of protection of individual rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007143_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007143-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmos20066_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmos20066-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1991 the momentum for incorporation had grown, garnering support from organisations such as <a href="/wiki/Charter88" class="mw-redirect" title="Charter88">Charter88</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberty_(pressure_group)" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberty (pressure group)">Liberty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Public_Policy_Research" title="Institute for Public Policy Research">Institute for Public Policy Research</a> - the latter two having published proposals for a British Bill of Rights incorporating the rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200279_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200279-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.56_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.56-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Incorporation was also advocated by senior members of the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a>, both past and present, including <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Gardiner,_Baron_Gardiner" title="Gerald Gardiner, Baron Gardiner">Lord Gardiner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quintin_Hogg,_Baron_Hailsham_of_St_Marylebone" title="Quintin Hogg, Baron Hailsham of St Marylebone">Lord Hailsham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Taylor,_Baron_Taylor_of_Gosforth" title="Peter Taylor, Baron Taylor of Gosforth">Lord Taylor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bingham,_Baron_Bingham_of_Cornhill" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Bingham, Baron Bingham of Cornhill">Lord Bingham</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/Law_Society_of_England_and_Wales" title="Law Society of England and Wales">Law Society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/General_Council_of_the_Bar" title="General Council of the Bar">Bar Council</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bringing_Rights_Home">Bringing Rights Home</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bringing Rights Home">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1992" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 1992">election</a> of <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a> as Labour Party leader in 1992, Labour policy towards the Convention - which had been against it before and during the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">1992 General Election</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007141_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007141-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> - began to change in favour of incorporation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman200278_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman200278-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.57_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.57-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Smith gave a speech on 1 March 1993 entitled "A Citizen\'s Democracy" in which he called for a "new constitutional settlement, a new deal between the people and the state that puts the citizen centre stage".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Party (UK) Conference">Labour Party Conference</a> in October 1993 which agreed in a two-stage policy whereby the Convention would be incorporated into law followed by the enactment of a Bill of Rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> It was planned to entrench Convention rights using a "notwithstanding clause" similar to that in <a href="/wiki/Section_Thirty-three_of_the_Canadian_Charter_of_Rights_and_Freedoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Section Thirty-three of the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms">Section Thirty-three of the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms</a>, which would have led to the Convention prevailing over legislation passed by Parliament unless stated otherwise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> A human rights commission would also be set up to monitor and promote human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.22-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1994 Lord Lester introduced a bill in the Lords which was based on the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Bill_of_Rights_Act_1990" title="New Zealand Bill of Rights Act 1990">New Zealand Bill of Rights</a> which would give the Convention a similar status in UK law as that accorded to <a href="/wiki/European_Community_law" class="mw-redirect" title="European Community law">European Community law</a> by allowing courts to disapply future and existing <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Acts of Parliament">Acts of Parliament</a> which were incompatible with it, imposing a duty on public authorities to comply and making provision for effective remedies including damages for breaches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.23_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.23-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Introduced during a period of concern over the impact of European Community law on the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliamentary sovereignty in the United Kingdom">Parliamentary sovereignty</a>, the bill did not receive support from the <a href="/wiki/Second_Major_ministry" title="Second Major ministry">Conservative government</a> and failed in the Commons due to lack of time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.23_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.23-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.58_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._1.58-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 18 December 1996, the shadow Labour Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Boateng" title="Paul Boateng">Paul Boateng</a> published a Consultation Paper headed "Bringing Rights Home" which set out Labour\'s plans to incorporate the Convention if it won the next election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.24_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.24-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The paper focused on the first stage of Labour\'s human rights policy and how the Convention should be incorporated, notably as regards its ability to override <a href="/wiki/Statutory_law" title="Statutory law">statutory law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.24_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.24-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> On 5 March 1997 a Labour and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a> Consultative Committee on Constitutional Reform chaired by <a href="/wiki/Robin_Cook" title="Robin Cook">Robin Cook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Maclennan,_Baron_Maclennan_of_Rogart" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Maclennan, Baron Maclennan of Rogart">Robert Maclennan</a> published a report calling for the creation of a "Human Rights Commissioner" to oversee the operation of the legislation and to bring cases on behalf of those seeking protection of their rights.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Passage_of_the_bill_through_Parliament">Passage of the bill through Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Passage of the bill through Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1997" title="United Kingdom general election, 1997">election of Labour</a> in May 1997 led to the publication of a <a href="/wiki/White_paper" title="White paper">white paper</a> on the bill - "Rights Brought Home: The Human Rights Bill".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007160_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007160-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The bill was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Derry_Irvine,_Baron_Irvine_of_Lairg" title="Derry Irvine, Baron Irvine of Lairg">Lord Irvine</a> to the House of Lords on 3 November 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In response to a question from <a href="/wiki/Jocelyn_Simon,_Baron_Simon_of_Glaisdale" class="mw-redirect" title="Jocelyn Simon, Baron Simon of Glaisdale">Lord Simon</a>, Lord Irvine confirmed that the bill did not in fact incorporate the Convention, but rather gave "further effect in the United Kingdom to convention rights".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Lord Irvine also rejected a proposed amendment by the Conservative <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Prout,_Baron_Kingsland" title="Christopher Prout, Baron Kingsland">Lord Kingsland</a> which would have had the effect of obliging the domestic courts to apply the <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">case-law</a> of the European Court of Human Rights, stating that the obligation to take it into account was sufficient.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The Liberal Democrats supported the bill, as did several <a href="/wiki/Crossbencher" title="Crossbencher">crossbenchers</a> including Lord Bingham, Lord Scarman, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wilberforce,_Baron_Wilberforce" title="Richard Wilberforce, Baron Wilberforce">Lord Wilberforce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Ackner,_Baron_Ackner" title="Desmond Ackner, Baron Ackner">Lord Ackner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robin_Cooke,_Baron_Cooke_of_Thorndon" title="Robin Cooke, Baron Cooke of Thorndon">Lord Cooke</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Donaldson,_Baron_Donaldson_of_Lymington" title="John Donaldson, Baron Donaldson of Lymington">Lord Donaldson</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The bill was opposed by the Conservative Party,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007143_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007143-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> although some <a href="/wiki/Backbencher" title="Backbencher">backbenchers</a> rebelled against the party line, most notably <a href="/wiki/David_Renton,_Baron_Renton" class="mw-redirect" title="David Renton, Baron Renton">Lord Renton</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Hennessy,_3rd_Baron_Windlesham" title="David Hennessy, 3rd Baron Windlesham">Lord Windlesham</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The second reading in the House of Commons took place on 16 February 1998 where the bill was introduced by Jack Straw who stated that it was "the first major Bill on human rights for more than 300 years".<sup id="cite_ref-Hansard-767_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansard-767-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the incorporation of the Convention in domestic law, Jack Straw indicated that the bill was intended to have two other significant effects: the <a href="/wiki/Modernization_theory" title="Modernization theory">modernisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratization" title="Democratization">democratisation</a> of the political system by bringing about "a better balance between rights and responsibilities, between the powers of the state and the freedom of the individual"<sup id="cite_ref-Hansard-767_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hansard-767-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and the introduction of a culture of awareness of human rights by public authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlug1999246-247_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlug1999246-247-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Amendments rejected included giving courts a greater degree of flexibility with regard to Strasbourg case-law and referring to the <a href="/wiki/Margin_of_appreciation" title="Margin of appreciation">margin of appreciation</a> accorded to states by the Court,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> as well as limiting the obligation to interpret legislation compatibly with Convention rights only when it was \'reasonable\' to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> A successful amendment was tabled by Labour backbencher <a href="/wiki/Kevin_McNamara_(politician)" title="Kevin McNamara (politician)">Kevin McNamara</a> which had the effect of incorporating into the bill Articles 1 and 2 of Protocol 6 of the Convention <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Capital punishment in the United Kingdom">abolishing the death penalty</a> in peacetime.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlug1999272-273_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlug1999272-273-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The bill successfully negotiated the Commons and the Lords and entered into force on 2 October 2000 as the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007160_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007160-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_Rights_Act_1998">Human Rights Act 1998</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Human Rights Act 1998">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_provisions">Key provisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Key provisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Act seeks to give direct effect to the European Convention on Human Rights in domestic law by enabling claimants to bring an action in national courts instead of having to take their case before the European Court of Human Rights, as had previously been the case.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmos20068_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmos20068-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The Act makes it unlawful for a public authority to act in a manner contrary to certain rights prescribed by the Convention<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.15_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.15-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and allows a <a href="/wiki/Courts_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Courts of the United Kingdom">UK court</a> to award a remedy in the event of a breach.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.23_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.23-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In principle, the Act has <a href="/wiki/Vertical_effect" title="Vertical effect">vertical effect</a> in that it operates only vis-\xc3\xa0-vis public bodies and not private parties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.05_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.05-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> There are however certain situations in which the Act can be indirectly invoked against a private person.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.72_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.72-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Section_3_of_the_Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Section 3 of the Human Rights Act 1998">Section 3 of the Act</a> requires primary and secondary legislation to be given effect in a way which is compatible with the Convention insofar as this is possible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.11_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.11-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> If the legislation cannot be interpreted in a manner which is compatible, this does not affect its validity, continuing operation or enforcement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.14_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._3.14-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In such a situation, <a href="/wiki/Sections_4_and_10_of_the_Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Sections 4 and 10 of the Human Rights Act 1998">section 4 of the Act</a> allows a court to make a <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_incompatibility" title="Declaration of incompatibility">declaration of incompatibility</a> which has no direct effect on the legislation nor any practical consequences for the case in which it is made;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._4.91_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._4.91-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> it allows Parliament to take remedial action without being obliged to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._4.91_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._4.91-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this article is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom##" title="Talk:Human rights in the United Kingdom">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span>  <small><i>(January 2015)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Since its entry into force, the Human Rights Act 1998 has been the focus of intense criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.49_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.49-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> It is blamed for decisions which are seen as privileging the rights of <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">criminals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011paras._1.39_and_1.49_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011paras._1.39_and_1.49-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In particular, cases where the <a href="/wiki/Deportation" title="Deportation">deportation</a> of criminals has been prevented on the basis of the risk posed to their human rights in the country to which they are to be sent has led to calls by politicians to "rebalance" the Act in favour of <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.49_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.49-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In July 2006, the Home Office published a report calling for the rights of law-abiding citizens to take precedence over those of criminals.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Senior politicians<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Media_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Media of the United Kingdom">British media</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> have criticised this aspect and the lenient treatment of criminals which the Act is said to encourage.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The Act has also been seen as hampering effective <a href="/wiki/Counter-terrorism" title="Counter-terrorism">counter-terrorism</a> action.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007164_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenwick2007164-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2006_Afghan_hijackers_case" title="2006 Afghan hijackers case">2006 Afghan hijackers case</a> in which a group of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghan</a> men who hijacked an aircraft in order to enter the UK were granted leave to remain in the UK was criticised in the British media<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and by both opposition and government politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> himself challenged the value of the Convention and the Act in the wake of news that a large number of foreign prisoners were released without being considered for deportation.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Act has been criticised by judges<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> on the basis that it does not incorporate all the rights in the Convention into domestic law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmos20069_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmos20069-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Legal commentators have also pointed out that the Convention is not a modern human rights instrument and was drafted in the 1940s as a response to specific historical circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._IN.16_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._IN.16-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The values which it embodies are said to be those of a different generation and omits in particular <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_rights" title="Children&#39;s rights">children\'s rights</a>, information rights and <a href="/wiki/Economic,_social_and_cultural_rights" title="Economic, social and cultural rights">socio-economic rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.25_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown2011para._1.25-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Senior judges<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> have called for a more critical approach.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_replacement_by_a_Bill_of_Rights">Proposed replacement by a Bill of Rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Proposed replacement by a Bill of Rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After <a href="/wiki/Learco_Chindamo" class="mw-redirect" title="Learco Chindamo">Learco Chindamo</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian</a> national convicted of the 1996 murder of a <a href="/wiki/Head_teacher" title="Head teacher">headteacher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Philip_Lawrence" title="Murder of Philip Lawrence">Philip Lawrence</a>, could not be deported to Italy after his release from prison in 2007, then-Conservative opposition leader <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> called for the repeal of the Act and its replacement by a "British Bill of Rights".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In July 2007, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> published a <a href="/wiki/Green_paper" title="Green paper">green paper</a> exploring the possibility of a Bill of Rights as part of a programme of constitutional reform.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In March 2009, a consultation process was launched with the publication of a <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Justice_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Justice (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Justice</a> green paper setting out preliminary proposals for a "Bill of Rights and Responsibilities".<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The consultation was not completed before the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown20111.51_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown20111.51-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_%E2%80%93_Liberal_Democrat_coalition_agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative \xe2\x80\x93 Liberal Democrat coalition agreement">Conservative \xe2\x80\x93 Liberal Democrat coalition agreement</a> provided for the establishment of a Commission to investigate the creation of a Bill of Rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown20111.52_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWadhamMountfieldProchaskaBrown20111.52-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The Commission was set up in March 2011. The nine-member Commission reported back in December 2012 that it had been unable to reach a consensus, with <a href="/wiki/Edward_Faulks,_Baron_Faulks" title="Edward Faulks, Baron Faulks">Lord Faulks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Fisher_(lawyer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jonathan Fisher (lawyer)">Jonathan Fisher</a>, the two Conservative members, in favour of withdrawal from the Convention, while <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Sands" title="Philippe Sands">Philippe Sands</a>, a Liberal Democrat adviser, and <a href="/wiki/Helena_Kennedy,_Baroness_Kennedy_of_The_Shaws" title="Helena Kennedy, Baroness Kennedy of The Shaws">Baroness Kennedy</a>, a Labour peer, opposed the introduction of a bill on the basis that it could be used to remove the United Kingdom from the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-18122012_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-18122012-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The Commission did agree however that no action should be taken until after the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">Scottish independence referendum</a>, at which point a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_meeting)" title="Constitutional convention (political meeting)">constitution convention</a> should be held.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian-18122012_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-18122012-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a speech at the 2013 Conservative Party conference, Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> said the Conservative Party would, if re-elected at the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 election</a>, withdraw from the Court and repeal the Human Rights Act if necessary to "fix" Britain\'s human rights laws.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> This followed a similar pledge by Justice Secretary <a href="/wiki/Chris_Grayling" title="Chris Grayling">Chris Grayling</a> in March 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland_Act_1998">Scotland Act 1998</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Scotland Act 1998">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Human rights in Scotland are given legal effect through the <a href="/wiki/Scotland_Act_1998" title="Scotland Act 1998">Scotland Act 1998</a>. Section 57(2) of the Act states: \xe2\x80\x9cA member of the Scottish Executive has no power to make any subordinate legislation, or to do any other act, so far as the legislation or act is incompatible with any of the Convention rights\xe2\x80\xa6\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Convention_rights_in_domestic_law">Convention rights in domestic law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Convention rights in domestic law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_life">Right to life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Right to life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_life" title="Right to life">Right to life</a></div>\n<p>The common law ensures the protection of the right to life and that no person is deprived of life intentionally.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.08_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.08-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> This is achieved primarily through the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a> and the crimes of <a href="/wiki/Murder_in_English_law" title="Murder in English law">murder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manslaughter_in_English_law" title="Manslaughter in English law">manslaughter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._106_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._106-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Some protection is also offered by the civil law where, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Fatal_Accidents_Act_1976" title="Fatal Accidents Act 1976">Fatal Accidents Act 1976</a> allows relatives of those killed by the wrongdoing of others to recover damages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Capital punishment in the United Kingdom">Capital punishment</a> had by 1998 been abolished in respect of all offences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.20_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.20-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Under the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coroners_Act_1988&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Coroners Act 1988 (page does not exist)">Coroners Act 1988</a> there is a duty in certain circumstances for deaths to be investigated by a <a href="/wiki/Coroner" title="Coroner">coroner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.44_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.44-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The law also attaches importance to the preservation of life: aiding and abetting a <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> is a criminal offence under the <a href="/wiki/Suicide_Act_1961" title="Suicide Act 1961">Suicide Act 1961</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a> is unlawful (see the <a href="/wiki/Tony_Bland#The_case" title="Tony Bland">Bland case</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.09_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.09-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, there is a duty upon medical professionals to keep patients alive unless to do so would be contrary to the patient\'s best interests based on professional medical opinion (the <a href="/wiki/Bolam_v_Friern_Hospital_Management_Committee" title="Bolam v Friern Hospital Management Committee">Bolam Test</a>), taking into account their quality of life in the event that treatment is continued.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.24_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._7.24-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Abortion_Act_1967" title="Abortion Act 1967">Abortion Act 1967</a> permits the termination of a pregnancy under certain conditions and the <a href="/wiki/Human_Fertilisation_and_Embryology_Act_1990" title="Human Fertilisation and Embryology Act 1990">Human Fertilisation and Embryology Act 1990</a> requires the storage of embryos to be licensed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._7.16_and_7.19_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._7.16_and_7.19-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is also an obligation on the state to prevent <a href="/wiki/Extreme_poverty" title="Extreme poverty">destitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neglect" title="Neglect">neglect</a> by providing relief to persons who may otherwise <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starve to death</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._5.130_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._5.130-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Freedom_of_thought.2C_conscience_and_religion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_thought,_conscience_and_religion">Freedom of thought, conscience and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Freedom of thought, conscience and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">Freedom of
