thought</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Freedom of religion in the United Kingdom">Freedom of religion in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The common law required punishment for "erroneous opinions concerning rites or modes of worship" to be provided for in legislation before it could be applied.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> There were a number of such laws in the 17th and 18th centuries, including the <a href="/wiki/Corporation_Act_1661" title="Corporation Act 1661">Corporation Act 1661</a> requiring holders of civic office to be members of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Test_Act_1673" class="mw-redirect" title="Test Act 1673">Test Act 1673</a> requiring holders of military or civil functions to take the oaths of supremacy and allegiance and subscribe to a declaration against <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantiation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Both Acts were repealed by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" title="Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829">Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829</a> which admitted <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholics</a> into the legal profession and permitted Catholic schools and places of worship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> were allowed to enter Parliament under the <a href="/wiki/Jews_Relief_Act_1858" title="Jews Relief Act 1858">Jews Relief Act 1858</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03_117-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.03-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_Crown_Act_2013" title="Succession to the Crown Act 2013">Succession to the Crown Act 2013</a> amended the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a> to remove the exclusion from the <a href="/wiki/Line_of_succession_to_the_British_throne" class="mw-redirect" title="Line of succession to the British throne">line of succession</a> of those who married Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> However, it remains the case that the Sovereign must be a member of the Church of England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.24_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.24-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a>, the Church of England is the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">established church</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and enjoys certain privileges and rights in law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.04_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.04-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> However, the promotion of <a href="/wiki/Anti-Christian_sentiment" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Christian sentiment">anti-Christian views</a> is no longer illegal and the law places no formal restrictions on the freedom of worship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.04_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.04-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> There is no formal legal definition of religion and courts generally abstain from deciding issues of religious <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.06_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.06-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The common law offences of <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy_law_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Blasphemy law in the United Kingdom">blasphemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blasphemous_libel" title="Blasphemous libel">blasphemous libel</a> were abolished by the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Justice_and_Immigration_Act_2008" title="Criminal Justice and Immigration Act 2008">Criminal Justice and Immigration Act 2008</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.18_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.18-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> A new offence of <a href="/wiki/Hate_speech" title="Hate speech">incitement to religious hatred</a> was created by the <a href="/wiki/Racial_and_Religious_Hatred_Act_2006" title="Racial and Religious Hatred Act 2006">Racial and Religious Hatred Act 2006</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.22_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.22-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and discrimination on the grounds of religion is regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Employment_Equality_(Religion_or_Belief)_Regulations_2003" title="Employment Equality (Religion or Belief) Regulations 2003">Employment Equality (Religion or Belief) Regulations 2003</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.26_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.26-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Military_Service_Act_1916" title="Military Service Act 1916">Military Service Act 1916</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Service_(Armed_Forces)_Act_1939" title="National Service (Armed Forces) Act 1939">National Service (Armed Forces) Act 1939</a> both provided for the possibility of exemption from military service on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Conscientious_objector" title="Conscientious objector">conscientious objection</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.23_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._14.23-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> although the House of Lords has held that there would be no breach of human rights if such a possibility was not provided for.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_expression">Freedom of expression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Freedom of expression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom of expression">Freedom of expression</a></div>\n<p>Regarded as one of the most important human rights,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._107_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._107-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> the courts have stated that there is no difference between the protection offered by the common law, and that guaranteed by the European Convention on Human Rights.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The right to freedom of expression has traditionally been considered as residual in character but certain types of speech are protected in positive terms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.07_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.07-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> This is the case of debates in Parliament which are protected by <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_privilege_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliamentary privilege in the United Kingdom">parliamentary privilege</a>, media reporting on parliamentary proceedings is covered by <a href="/wiki/Absolute_privilege_in_English_law" title="Absolute privilege in English law">absolute privilege</a>, while reports of court proceedings are protected by <a href="/wiki/Qualified_privilege" title="Qualified privilege">qualified privilege</a> so long as they are fair, accurate and not published with malice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.07_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.07-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The right of freedom of expression can be restricted where justified in the <a href="/wiki/Public_interest" title="Public interest">public interest</a>, as is the case with the <a href="/wiki/English_defamation_law" title="English defamation law">law of defamation</a>, the offence of <a href="/wiki/Contempt_of_court" title="Contempt of court">contempt of court</a>, the <a href="/wiki/English_criminal_law#Offences_against_public_morals_and_public_policy" title="English criminal law">law of obscenity and indecency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Censorship_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Censorship in the United Kingdom">regulation of mass media</a> and protection of <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.09_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.09-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_confidence_in_English_law" title="Breach of confidence in English law">Breach of confidence</a> may be invoked before a court to obtain an <a href="/wiki/Injunctions_in_English_law" title="Injunctions in English law">injunction</a> preventing the disclosure of confidential information.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.35_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._15.35-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_assembly_and_association">Freedom of assembly and association</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Freedom of assembly and association">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_assembly" title="Freedom of assembly">Freedom of assembly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_association" title="Freedom of association">Freedom of association</a></div>\n<p>The right to free assembly is considered an aspect of the right to freedom of expression.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._109_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._109-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Simply put by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Denning,_Baron_Denning" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred Denning, Baron Denning">Lord Denning</a>, "it is the right for everyone to meet and assemble with his fellows to discuss their affairs and to promote their views".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> It has traditionally been regarded as a residual right which individuals are free to exercise so long as the law does not preclude them from doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.06_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.06-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/A._V._Dicey" title="A. V. Dicey">Dicey</a>, "at no time has there in England been any proclamation of the right to liberty of thought or to freedom of speech, [...] it can hardly be said that our constitution knows of such a thing as any specific right of public meeting".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDicey1889239-240,_271_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDicey1889239-240,_271-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> There are no common law rights to hold meetings on public land or in public buildings and the owners of quasi-public spaces such as shopping centres may exclude members of the public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.10_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.10-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Peaceful meetings may be held on the <a href="/wiki/Highways_in_England_and_Wales" title="Highways in England and Wales">highway</a> so long as they do not restrict access by other road users.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The exercise of the right to free assembly is restricted by the common law as well as legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._109_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._109-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> There is a common law duty to protect the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_peace" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen&#39;s peace">Queen\'s peace</a> and the power to detain persons who commit <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_the_peace" title="Breach of the peace">breach of the peace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.13_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.13-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Public_Order_Act_1986" title="Public Order Act 1986">Public Order Act 1986</a> provides for the offences of <a href="/wiki/Riot" title="Riot">riot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Violent_disorder" title="Violent disorder">violent disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Affray" title="Affray">affray</a> and causing <a href="/wiki/Harassment,_alarm_or_distress" class="mw-redirect" title="Harassment, alarm or distress">harassment, alarm or distress</a>, all of which may be committed by participants in an assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.20_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.20-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Public_Order_Act_1936" title="Public Order Act 1936">Public Order Act 1936</a> outlaws the wearing of <a href="/wiki/Political_uniform" title="Political uniform">political uniforms</a> at a public meeting when they suggest an association with a political object.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._109_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._109-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Justice_and_Public_Order_Act_1994" title="Criminal Justice and Public Order Act 1994">Criminal Justice and Public Order Act 1994</a> gives the police powers in relation to <a href="/wiki/Trespass_in_English_law" title="Trespass in English law">trespassers</a> on private land.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.22_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.22-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup> There is now a requirement under the <a href="/wiki/Serious_Organised_Crime_and_Police_Act_2005" title="Serious Organised Crime and Police Act 2005">Serious Organised Crime and Police Act 2005</a> to notify the <a href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Police_of_the_Metropolis" title="Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis">Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis</a> of an intention to demonstrate in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Square" title="Parliament Square">Parliament Square</a> and the Commissioner may impose such conditions as they see fit, including the place, time and number of participants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.35_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.35-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As with the right of freedom of assembly, there has not traditionally been positive protection of the right of freedom of association.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.49_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.49-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The common law has recognised the autonomy of <a href="/wiki/Organization" title="Organization">organisations</a> in deciding their membership and legislation has not placed any general controls on the exercise of the right of association.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.49_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._16.49-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The right is generally reflected in the principle of <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_contract" title="Freedom of contract">freedom of contract</a> and the relative ease by which <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_company_law" title="United Kingdom company law">companies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trust_law_in_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Trust law in England and Wales">trusts</a> can be set up.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._117_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._117-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The right is most regulated in the area of <a href="/wiki/British_labour_law" class="mw-redirect" title="British labour law">labour relations</a> including the right to join a <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade union</a> which achieved statutory recognition in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Relations_Act_1971" title="Industrial Relations Act 1971">Industrial Relations Act 1971</a>, the right not to be dismissed or refused employment on the basis of non-membership under the <a href="/wiki/Trade_Union_and_Labour_Relations_(Consolidation)_Act_1992" title="Trade Union and Labour Relations (Consolidation) Act 1992">Trade Union and Labour Relations (Consolidation) Act 1992</a> and the right to claim <a href="/wiki/Unfair_dismissal_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Unfair dismissal in the United Kingdom">unfair dismissal</a> on the basis of trade union activity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._117_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._117-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> However, there is no obligation on employers to recognise <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining agreements</a> except in certain very limited circumstances and their role has declined significantly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Right_to_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="Right to strike">right to strike</a> was acknowledged for the first time in March 2011 by the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Appeal_of_England_and_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Court of Appeal of England and Wales">Court of Appeal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The right not to be unjustly excluded from the exercise of a trade or profession has been recognised by the courts, in a case brought by female horse trainer <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nagle" title="Florence Nagle">Florence Nagle</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Jockey_Club" title="Jockey Club">Jockey Club</a>\'s refusal to grant her a training licence on grounds of her sex.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The right of freedom of association can be restricted on grounds of public order and national security; the <a href="/wiki/Public_Order_Act_1936" title="Public Order Act 1936">Public Order Act 1936</a> prohibits the control or management of quasi-military associations and the <a href="/wiki/Terrorism_Act_2000" title="Terrorism Act 2000">Terrorism Act 2000</a> makes it an offence to be a member of a proscribed organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._16.67_and_16.69_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._16.67_and_16.69-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_respect_for_privacy_and_the_home">Right to respect for privacy and the home</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Right to respect for privacy and the home">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Privacy_in_English_law" title="Privacy in English law">Privacy in English law</a></div>\n<p>There is no general <a href="/wiki/Right_to_privacy" title="Right to privacy">right to privacy</a> in English law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.08_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.08-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Attempts to establish such a right in <a href="/wiki/Kaye_v_Robertson" title="Kaye v Robertson">Kaye v Robertson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wainwright_v_Home_Office" title="Wainwright v Home Office">Wainwright v Home Office</a> were rejected on the basis that it could only be done by Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._12.08-12.09_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._12.08-12.09-151">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The creation of a tort of infringement of privacy had been recommended by <a href="/wiki/David_Calcutt" title="David Calcutt">Sir David Calcutt</a> in his <i>Review of Press Self-Regulation</i> published in 1993,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> but no action was taken.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.10_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.10-153">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Instead, a patchwork of different torts combine to protect certain aspects of privacy,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._110_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._110-154">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> such as breach of confidence and <a href="/wiki/Misuse_of_private_information_in_English_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Misuse of private information in English law">misuse of private information</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.20_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.20-155">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Campbell_v_Mirror_Group_Newspapers_Ltd" class="mw-redirect" title="Campbell v Mirror Group Newspapers Ltd">Campbell v Mirror Group Newspapers Ltd</a>, the House of Lords confirmed that a person will be subject to a duty of confidence if they receive information in circumstances where there is a reasonable expectation of privacy and where there is no justification for interference with that right to privacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.24_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.24-156">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> This was followed by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_v_Hello!_Ltd" title="Douglas v Hello! Ltd">Douglas v Hello! Ltd</a> where a magazine was successfully sued for publishing unauthorised photographs of a celebrity wedding.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;nb 3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Data_Protection_Act_1998" title="Data Protection Act 1998">Data Protection Act 1998</a> protects certain <a href="/wiki/Personally_identifiable_information" title="Personally identifiable information">personal data</a> which must be processed in accordance with a number of principles and gives persons a right of access to data which concerns them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._12.63-12.65_and_12.76_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._12.63-12.65_and_12.76-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Legal_professional_privilege_in_England_and_Wales" title="Legal professional privilege in England and Wales">Legal privilege</a> protects communications with lawyers for the purposes of giving or obtaining legal advice and in the context of actual or contemplated litigation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.77A_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.77A-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The right to freedom from interference with personal property is a well-recognised principle reflected in the maxim "<a href="/wiki/An_Englishman%27s_home_is_his_castle" class="mw-redirect" title="An Englishman&#39;s home is his castle">an Englishman\'s home is his castle</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.117_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.117-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Entick_v_Carrington" title="Entick v Carrington">Entick v Carrington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Pratt,_1st_Earl_Camden" title="Charles Pratt, 1st Earl Camden">Lord Camden</a> famously asserted that seizure and retention of property will be unlawful unless justified by law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.118_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.118-162">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> It is an offence to use violence to obtain unauthorised access to a property under the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Law_Act_1977" title="Criminal Law Act 1977">Criminal Law Act 1977</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Protection_from_Eviction_Act_1977" title="Protection from Eviction Act 1977">Protection from Eviction Act 1977</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Applications for <a href="/wiki/Search_warrants" class="mw-redirect" title="Search warrants">search warrants</a> must comply with the procedure in the <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Criminal_Evidence_Act_1984" title="Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984">Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984</a> and police surveillance is covered in the <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_Investigatory_Powers_Act_2000" title="Regulation of Investigatory Powers Act 2000">Regulation of Investigatory Powers Act 2000</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._12.123_and_12.159_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._12.123_and_12.159-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Surveillance" title="Surveillance">Surveillance</a> by the security and intelligence services is provided for in the <a href="/wiki/Security_Service_Act_1989" title="Security Service Act 1989">Security Service Act 1989</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_Services_Act_1994" title="Intelligence Services Act 1994">Intelligence Services Act 1994</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.175_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.175-164">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The right to respect for the home relates only to an individual\'s existing home and there is no right to acquire a home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.182_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._12.182-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_marry_and_family_life">Right to marry and family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Right to marry and family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_England_and_Wales" title="Marriage in England and Wales">Marriage in England and Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Scotland" title="Marriage in Scotland">Marriage in Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Marriage in Northern Ireland">Marriage in Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<p>There is no general right to marry.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.04_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.04-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The necessary formalities in the <a href="/wiki/Marriage_Act_1949" title="Marriage Act 1949">Marriage Act 1949</a> must be observed if the marriage is to be valid and parties who are less than 18 years of age must obtain consent under the <a href="/wiki/Children_Act_1989" title="Children Act 1989">Children Act 1989</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.08_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.08-168">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Civil_Partnership_Act_2004" title="Civil Partnership Act 2004">Civil Partnership Act 2004</a> introduced the concept of <a href="/wiki/Civil_partnership_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Civil partnership in the United Kingdom">civil partnerships</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Marriage_(Same_Sex_Couples)_Act_2013" title="Marriage (Same Sex Couples) Act 2013">Marriage (Same Sex Couples) Act 2013</a> provides for <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Same-sex marriage in the United Kingdom">same-sex marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.14_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.14-169">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The right to obtain a <a href="/wiki/Divorce_in_England_and_Wales" title="Divorce in England and Wales">divorce</a> is contained in the <a href="/wiki/Matrimonial_Causes_Act_1973" title="Matrimonial Causes Act 1973">Matrimonial Causes Act 1973</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.15_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.15-171">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gender_Recognition_Act_2004" title="Gender Recognition Act 2004">Gender Recognition Act 2004</a> allows <a href="/wiki/Transsexual" title="Transsexual">transsexuals</a> to change their legal <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.12_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.12-172">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The right to respect for family life, for which there is no general right at common law,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.04_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.04-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> is qualified by the broad principle that the welfare of the child is paramount and <a href="/wiki/Parental_responsibility_(access_and_custody)" title="Parental responsibility (access and custody)">parental rights</a> must take second place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._113_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._113-173">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Gillick_v_West_Norfolk_Area_Health_Authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Gillick v West Norfolk Area Health Authority">Gillick v West Norfolk Area Health Authority</a>, Lord Scarman stated that "parental rights are derived from parental duty and exist only so long as they are needed for the protection of the person and property of the child", while <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fraser,_Baron_Fraser_of_Tullybelton" title="Ian Fraser, Baron Fraser of Tullybelton">Lord Fraser</a> said that "parental rights to control a child do not exist for the benefit of the parent".<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The effect of this is to allow state intervention in family life where justified in the interests of the child in question, and the <a href="/wiki/Children_Act_1989" title="Children Act 1989">Children Act 1989</a> gives effect to this by providing a basis on which decisions relating to a child\'s welfare are made.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._113_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._113-173">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Section 1 of the Act provides that a court must, when taking a decision with regard to a child, take into account the child\'s wishes and feelings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.25_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.25-175">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Adoption is regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Adoption_and_Children_Act_2002" title="Adoption and Children Act 2002">Adoption and Children Act 2002</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.60_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.60-176">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Local authorities have a duty to act in a way to facilitate the right to family life, for example in providing travel arrangements for the elderly under the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Health_Services_and_Public_Health_Act_1968&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Health Services and Public Health Act 1968 (page does not exist)">Health Services and Public Health Act 1968</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.109_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._13.109-177">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The right to education is guaranteed by the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1944" title="Education Act 1944">Education Act 1944</a>, and the right to housing is enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/Housing_Act_1985" title="Housing Act 1985">Housing Act 1985</a>. The enactment of the <a href="/wiki/National_Minimum_Wage_Act_1998" title="National Minimum Wage Act 1998">National Minimum Wage Act 1998</a> installed a <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_Contributions_and_Benefits_Act_1992" title="Social Security Contributions and Benefits Act 1992">Social Security Contributions and Benefits Act 1992</a> provides access to <a href="/wiki/Social_security" title="Social security">social security</a> benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._121_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._121-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> There is no positive right to healthcare; the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service_Act_1977" class="mw-redirect" title="National Health Service Act 1977">National Health Service Act 1977</a> imposes a duty on the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_(United_Kingdom)" title="Secretary of State (United Kingdom)">Secretary of State</a> to provide "adequate" healthcare but the courts have not thus far been willing to enforce this duty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._121_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._121-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prohibition_of_torture_or_inhuman_or_degrading_treatment">Prohibition of torture or inhuman or degrading treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Prohibition of torture or inhuman or degrading treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The common law recognises that every person\'s body is "inviolate".<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Interference will constitute the offence of <a href="/wiki/Common_assault" title="Common assault">common assault</a> and the tort of <a href="/wiki/Battery_(tort)" title="Battery (tort)">battery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.03_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.03-180">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Under the criminal law, an individual cannot consent to actual bodily harm and the House of Lords held in <a href="/wiki/R_v_Brown" title="R v Brown">R v Brown</a> that acts of <a href="/wiki/BDSM" title="BDSM">sado-masochism</a> committed in private between <a href="/wiki/Consent_(criminal_law)" title="Consent (criminal law)">consenting adults</a> did not give the author of the harm a valid defence in respect of his acts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.04_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.04-181">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Children_Act_2004" title="Children Act 2004">Children Act 2004</a> removed the defence of reasonable chastissement in respect of acts committed against children and <a href="/wiki/School_corporal_punishment" title="School corporal punishment">corporal punishment in schools</a> was outlawed in the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1996" title="Education Act 1996">Education Act 1996</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._8.09-8.10_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._8.09-8.10-182">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Justice_Act_1988" title="Criminal Justice Act 1988">Criminal Justice Act 1988</a> prohibits <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> carried out by public officials in the performance of their duties and evidence obtained by torture is excluded by the common law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._8.12_and_8.15_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._8.12_and_8.15-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a> outlawed <a href="/wiki/Cruel_and_unusual_punishment" title="Cruel and unusual punishment">cruel and unusual punishment</a> and provided that an action for damages could be brought by victims.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.16_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.16-184">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Criminal_Evidence_Act_1984" title="Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984">Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984</a> allows police to carry out "intimate searches" of suspects in custody without the person\'s consent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.42_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._8.42-185">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_enjoyment_of_possessions">Right to enjoyment of possessions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Right to enjoyment of possessions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">Right to property</a></div>\n<p>Protection against the arbitrary deprivation of property was recognised in the <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a> and is of key importance in the common law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._18.01_and_18.04_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._18.01_and_18.04-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Protection of the right to own and enjoy property is found in the offence of <a href="/wiki/Theft#Theft_in_English_law" title="Theft">theft</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Intellectual property law">intellectual property laws</a> and by the principle that there can be no <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxation</a> except that which is authorised by Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._111_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._111-187">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The right to property is qualified by <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_purchase_in_England_and_Wales" title="Compulsory purchase in England and Wales">compulsory purchase laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._18.18_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._18.18-188">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In civil cases, a judge may grant an <a href="/wiki/Anton_Piller_order" title="Anton Piller order">Anton Piller order</a> authorising the search of premises and seizure of evidence without prior warning.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The order\'s purpose is to prevent the destruction of incriminating evidence, particularly in cases of alleged <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property_infringement" title="Intellectual property infringement">intellectual property infringement</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_from_slavery_and_forced_labour">Freedom from slavery and forced labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Freedom from slavery and forced labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_at_common_law" title="Slavery at common law">Slavery at common law</a></div>\n<p>Slavery was abolished throughout much of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">Slavery Abolition Act 1833</a> but as early as 1706 the common law had recognised that as soon as a slave came to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, he became free.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._9.06_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._9.06-190">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The courts did not recognise contracts which were equivalent to slavery or servitude.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._9.06_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._9.06-190">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The courts have been reluctant to force individuals to work and have declined to enforce <a href="/wiki/Employment_contract" title="Employment contract">contracts of service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._9.07_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._9.07-191">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">Forced labour</a> is however permitted in certain limited circumstances: the Crown can theoretically compel persons to undertake <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Naval_Service" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Naval Service">naval service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prisoner" title="Prisoner">prisoners</a> can be forced to work whilst serving their sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._9.08-9.09_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._9.08-9.09-192">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Supreme Court of the United Kingdom">Supreme Court of the United Kingdom</a> considered the forced labour prohibition in <a href="/wiki/Caitlin_Reilly_and_Jamieson_Wilson_v_Secretary_of_State_for_Work_and_Pensions" class="mw-redirect" title="Caitlin Reilly and Jamieson Wilson v Secretary of State for Work and Pensions">Caitlin Reilly and Jamieson Wilson v Secretary of State for Work and Pensions</a>, where it ruled that the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Work_and_Pensions" title="Department for Work and Pensions">Department for Work and Pensions</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Workfare_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Workfare in the United Kingdom">workfare policy</a> under which the <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployed</a> work in return for their <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_benefits" title="Unemployment benefits">benefit payments</a> were not forced labour but were nevertheless legally flawed.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_liberty">Right to liberty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Right to liberty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_liberty" class="mw-redirect" title="Right to liberty">Right to liberty</a></div>\n<p>As provided for in the <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, "no freeman shall be taken, imprisoned [...] except by the lawful judgment of his peers and by the law of the land."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.01_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.01-194">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The right is protected in three ways: the writ of <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a> requires a person under arrest to be brought before a judge or into court, the tort of <a href="/wiki/False_imprisonment" title="False imprisonment">false imprisonment</a> and the offence of <a href="/wiki/Kidnapping" title="Kidnapping">kidnapping</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.07_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.07-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._115_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._115-196">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Where an individual is lawfully arrested on the basis of an <a href="/wiki/Arrest_warrant" title="Arrest warrant">arrest warrant</a>, the length of his detention is prescribed by statute; the detainee\'s rights and the powers of the police during the period of detention are also regulated under the <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Criminal_Evidence_Act_1984" title="Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984">Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.25_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.25-197">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The police have no general power to detain a person for <a href="/wiki/Interrogation" title="Interrogation">questioning</a> without making an arrest unless this is for the purpose of the exercise of another police power such as the use of a search warrant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.27_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.27-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arrest_without_warrant" title="Arrest without warrant">Arrests without a warrant</a> are possible if the person is committing or about to commit an offence and the arresting officer has reasonable grounds to believe that arrest is necessary in order to ascertain the person\'s details, to prevent them from causing injury, damage or further offences, to protect a child or other vulnerable person, to allow prompt and effective investigation of the offence or the person\'s conduct or to prevent the investigation being hindered by the person\'s disappearance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.38_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.38-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The police also have <a href="/wiki/Powers_of_the_police_in_England_and_Wales" title="Powers of the police in England and Wales">stop and search powers</a> which they can use if they have reasonable grounds to believe that they will find stolen or prohibited articles or <a href="/wiki/Controlled_Drug_in_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Controlled Drug in United Kingdom">controlled drugs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.28_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.28-200">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Justice_and_Public_Order_Act_1994" title="Criminal Justice and Public Order Act 1994">Criminal Justice and Public Order Act 1994</a>, a senior police officer can authorise police officers in a locality to stop and search any pedestrian or vehicle where the officer has grounds for believing that the individual is carrying an <a href="/wiki/Offensive_weapon" title="Offensive weapon">offensive weapon</a> or dangerous instrument.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.29_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.29-201">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> In 1998 this legislation was extended to allow the officer to require the person to remove clothing worn for the purpose of concealing his identity, and to confiscate that article of clothing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Special extended powers also apply in the case of terrorist suspects.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A person may be detained without charge for up to 24 hours, which can be extended for a further 12 hours by a senior officer in the case of a serious arrestable offence and further extended by a <a href="/wiki/Magistrates%27_court_(England_and_Wales)" title="Magistrates&#39; court (England and Wales)">magistrates\' court</a> up to a total of 60 hours in respect of <a href="/wiki/Indictable_offence" title="Indictable offence">indictable offences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.59_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._10.59-202">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Suspected terrorists may be detained for up to 48 hours without charge and <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigrants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Refugee#Asylum_seekers" title="Refugee">asylum-seekers</a> may be detained for examination by immigration officers for an unlimited period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.63_and_10.65_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.63_and_10.65-203">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Certain other categories of person may also be detained, such as persons in need of care and attention under the <a href="/wiki/National_Assistance_Act_1948" title="National Assistance Act 1948">National Assistance Act 1948</a>, children in the care of a <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_England" title="Local government in England">local authority</a> in <a href="/wiki/Secure_units_for_children" title="Secure units for children">secure accommodation</a>, those suffering from infectious diseases under the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Public_Health_(Control_of_Diseases)_Act_1984&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Public Health (Control of Diseases) Act 1984 (page does not exist)">Public Health (Control of Diseases) Act 1984</a> and those detained by the <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">British Armed Forces</a> abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.76-10.80_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.76-10.80-204">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The courts have powers to <a href="/wiki/Sentencing_in_England_and_Wales" title="Sentencing in England and Wales">sentence</a> offenders and deprive them of their liberty, as well as detain mental patients under the provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Mental_Health_Act_1983" title="Mental Health Act 1983">Mental Health Act 1983</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.95_and_10.122_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.95_and_10.122-205">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> They may also order the payment of compensation to persons who have been unlawfully detained and the Home Secretary may award payments to victims of <a href="/wiki/Miscarriages_of_justice" class="mw-redirect" title="Miscarriages of justice">miscarriages of justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.143_and_10.146_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._10.143_and_10.146-206">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights_of_fair_trial">Rights of fair trial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Rights of fair trial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_a_fair_trial" title="Right to a fair trial">Right to a fair trial</a></div>\n<p>Although the common law provides no explicit statement of rights in a legal process, a number of principles combine to guarantee an individual a certain level of protection by law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._119_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._119-207">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.06_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.06-208">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> These are often known as the rules of <a href="/wiki/Natural_justice" title="Natural justice">natural justice</a> and comprise the principles <a href="/wiki/Nemo_iudex_in_causa_sua" title="Nemo iudex in causa sua">nemo iudex in causa sua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Audi_alteram_partem" title="Audi alteram partem">audi alteram partem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.124_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.124-209">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> A fair <a href="/wiki/Hearing_(law)" title="Hearing (law)">hearing</a> implies that each party has the opportunity to present their own case to an impartial tribunal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.166_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.166-210">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Courts must generally sit in public and decisions may be challenged on the grounds of actual or apparent <a href="/wiki/Bias" title="Bias">bias</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._11.142_and_11.181_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._11.142_and_11.181-211">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Although judges are under a common law duty to give reasons for their decisions, there is no such rule for non-judicial decision-makers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._11.205-11.206_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._11.205-11.206-212">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Certain specific rights apply in criminal cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.210_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.210-213">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Persons in custody have the right to consult a solicitor by virtue of the <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Criminal_Evidence_Act_1984" title="Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984">Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Access_to_Justice_Act_1999" title="Access to Justice Act 1999">Access to Justice Act 1999</a> allows an individual to request confidential and <a href="/wiki/Legal_aid#Legal_aid_in_England_and_Wales" title="Legal aid">free</a> legal advice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._11.215_and_11.217_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._11.215_and_11.217-214">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The prosecution is under a common law duty to disclose all relevant evidence including that which may assist the defendant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.221_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.221-215">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The accused also benefits from a limited <a href="/wiki/Right_to_silence_in_England_and_Wales" title="Right to silence in England and Wales">right to silence</a> and the privilege against <a href="/wiki/Self-incrimination" title="Self-incrimination">self-incrimination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.231_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.231-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Confession_(law)" title="Confession (law)">Confessions</a> are admissible as evidence under certain conditions laid down in the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.250_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.250-217">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> The accused additionally benefits from a <a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">presumption of innocence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.253_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.253-218">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> In discharging the <a href="/wiki/Legal_burden_of_proof" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal burden of proof">burden of proof</a>, the onus is on the prosecution as established in <a href="/wiki/Woolmington_v_DPP" title="Woolmington v DPP">Woolmington v DPP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.254_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.254-219">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> For example, in <a href="/wiki/R_v_Wang" title="R v Wang">R v Wang</a> it was held that it was never permissible for a judge to direct a jury to find a defendant guilty.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> There is also a right to <a href="/wiki/Juries_in_England_and_Wales" title="Juries in England and Wales">jury trial</a>, the accused should be able to follow proceedings and they have a right to be present during proceedings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.262,_11.270_and_11.272_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.262,_11.270_and_11.272-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> The application of the <a href="/wiki/Double_jeopardy" title="Double jeopardy">double jeopardy</a> rule was limited by the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Justice_Act_2003" title="Criminal Justice Act 2003">Criminal Justice Act 2003</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Courts have jurisdiction to prevent <a href="/wiki/Abuse_of_process" title="Abuse of process">abuses of process</a> if it would be unfair to try a defendant or a fair trial would not be possible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.302_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._11.302-222">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_from_discrimination">Freedom from discrimination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Freedom from discrimination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_from_discrimination" title="Freedom from discrimination">Freedom from discrimination</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_employment_equality_law" title="United Kingdom employment equality law">United Kingdom employment equality law</a></div>\n<p>The common law has not traditionally provided effective protection against <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a>, refusing to find that slavery in the colonies was contrary to public policy and not interpreting the word "person" to include women.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.06_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.06-223">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Due to the inadequacies of the common law in this area, Parliament enacted the <a href="/wiki/Race_Relations_Act_1965" title="Race Relations Act 1965">Race Relations Act 1965</a> and <a href="/wiki/Race_Relations_Act_1968" title="Race Relations Act 1968">Race Relations Act 1968</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.09_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.09-224">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sex_Discrimination_Act_1975" title="Sex Discrimination Act 1975">Sex Discrimination Act 1975</a> was the first legislation to protect against discrimination on the grounds of sex or marital status,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.18_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.18-225">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> while dismissal for pregnancy-related reasons is qualified as automatically <a href="/wiki/Unfair_dismissal_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Unfair dismissal in the United Kingdom">unfair dismissal</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Employment_Rights_Act_1996" title="Employment Rights Act 1996">Employment Rights Act 1996</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.25_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.25-226">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> The principle of <a href="/wiki/Equal_pay" class="mw-redirect" title="Equal pay">equal pay</a> was brought in under the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Pay_Act_1970" title="Equal Pay Act 1970">Equal Pay Act 1970</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.36_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.36-227">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">Racial discrimination</a> was prohibited by the <a href="/wiki/Race_Relations_Act_1976" title="Race Relations Act 1976">Race Relations Act 1976</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disability_discrimination" class="mw-redirect" title="Disability discrimination">disability discrimination</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Disability_Discrimination_Act_1995" title="Disability Discrimination Act 1995">Disability Discrimination Act 1995</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._17.44_and_17.65_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._17.44_and_17.65-228">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Discrimination on the grounds of religion and belief in the workplace and tertiary education was first regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Employment_Equality_(Religion_or_Belief)_Regulations_2003" title="Employment Equality (Religion or Belief) Regulations 2003">Employment Equality (Religion or Belief) Regulations 2003</a>, sexual orientation discrimination in the workplace by the <a href="/wiki/Employment_Equality_(Sexual_Orientation)_Regulations_2003" title="Employment Equality (Sexual Orientation) Regulations 2003">Employment Equality (Sexual Orientation) Regulations 2003</a> and <a href="/wiki/Age_discrimination" class="mw-redirect" title="Age discrimination">age discrimination</a> in employment by the <a href="/wiki/Employment_Equality_(Age)_Regulations_2006" title="Employment Equality (Age) Regulations 2006">Employment Equality (Age) Regulations 2006</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._17.81,_17.92_and_17.104_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._17.81,_17.92_and_17.104-229">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much of this legislation was consolidated into the <a href="/wiki/Equality_Act_2010" title="Equality Act 2010">Equality Act 2010</a>, the bulk of which entered into force in October 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> which also introduces an "equality duty" requiring public bodies to have due regard to the need to eliminate discrimination, to advance equality of opportunity and to foster good relations between people.<sup id="cite_ref-EA-Guidance_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA-Guidance-231">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> It also imposes a duty on public bodies to publish information about compliance with the equality duty and to set equality objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-EA-Guidance_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EA-Guidance-231">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the Act clarifies existing legislation and simplifies the definition of direct discrimination, extends the definitions of indirect discrimination and harassment, regulates pay secrecy clauses in employment contracts and the use of health questionnaires by employers.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-08102010_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-08102010-232">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> It also gives <a href="/wiki/Employment_tribunal" title="Employment tribunal">employment tribunals</a> new powers to make recommendations to employers.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-08102010_232-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-08102010-232">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_rights">Electoral rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Electoral rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Elections in the United Kingdom">Elections in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1983" title="Representation of the People Act 1983">Representation of the People Acts 1983</a> and <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_2000" title="Representation of the People Act 2000">2000</a> confer the <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">franchise</a> on <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">British subjects</a> and citizens of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> who are resident in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.25_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._17.25-226">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._118_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._118-233">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> In addition, nationals of other <a href="/wiki/Member_States_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Member States of the European Union">Member States of the European Union</a> have the right to vote in <a href="/wiki/Local_election" title="Local election">local elections</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elections_to_the_European_Parliament" title="Elections to the European Parliament">elections to the European Parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._118_233-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._118-233">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> The right to vote also includes the right to a <a href="/wiki/Secret_ballot" title="Secret ballot">secret ballot</a> and the right to stand as a candidate in elections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._118_233-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesterOliver1997para._118-233">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Certain persons are excluded from participation including <a href="/wiki/Peerage" title="Peerage">peers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alien_(law)" title="Alien (law)">aliens</a>, infants, persons of unsound mind, holders of judicial office, <a href="/wiki/Civil_servants" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil servants">civil servants</a>, members of the regular armed forces or police, members of any non-Commonwealth legislature, members of various commissions, boards and tribunals, persons imprisoned for more than one year, <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Bankruptcy in the United Kingdom">bankrupts</a> and persons convicted of corrupt or illegal election practices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._20.11_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._20.11-234">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> The restriction on the participation of <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a> was removed by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_(Removal_of_Clergy_Disqualification)_Act_2001" title="House of Commons (Removal of Clergy Disqualification) Act 2001">House of Commons (Removal of Clergy Disqualification) Act 2001</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._20.11_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._20.11-234">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Political_Parties,_Elections_and_Referendums_Act_2000" title="Political Parties, Elections and Referendums Act 2000">Political Parties, Elections and Referendums Act 2000</a> deals with the registration of <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of political parties in the United Kingdom">political parties</a>, while <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_constituencies" title="United Kingdom constituencies">electoral boundaries</a> are determined by four <a href="/wiki/Boundary_Commissions_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Boundary Commissions (United Kingdom)">Boundary Committees</a> established under the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Constituencies_Act_1986" title="Parliamentary Constituencies Act 1986">Parliamentary Constituencies Act 1986</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._20.12_and_20.13_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009paras._20.12_and_20.13-235">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Electoral campaigns and electoral publicity, including expenditure rules per candidate, are regulated by the Political Parties, Elections and Referendums Act 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._20.17_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClaytonTomlinson2009para._20.17-236">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights_conferred_by_European_Union_law">Rights conferred by European Union law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Rights conferred by European Union law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Fundamental_rights" title="Fundamental rights">Fundamental rights</a> form an integral part of <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Union law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._17-077_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._17-077-237">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> As such, the principle of <a href="/wiki/Supremacy_(European_Union_law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Supremacy (European Union law)">primacy of European Union law</a> requires that any action taken by its <a href="/wiki/Member_States_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Member States of the European Union">member states</a> must comply with the requirements of EU law as to the protection of fundamental rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._17-077_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._17-077-237">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Fundamental_Rights_of_the_European_Union" title="Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union">Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union</a>, which has legal effect equivalent to the Treaties following the entry into force of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> is applicable to Member States when "they are implementing Union law".<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The Charter includes a number of rights not provided for in the Convention including the right of access to healthcare, the right of collective bargaining and action, the right to freedom of the arts and sciences, the right to education and the right of access to a free placement service.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The United Kingdom sought to obtain a partial <a href="/wiki/Opt-outs_in_the_European_Union" title="Opt-outs in the European Union">opt-out</a> from the application of the Charter with the addition of Protocol 30 which affirms that the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> does not have the jurisdiction to state a breach of the Charter by national laws, regulations or administrative provisions, practices or action, and that nothing in Title IV of the Charter concerning employment rights creates "<a href="/wiki/Justiciability" title="Justiciability">justiciable rights</a>" applicable to the United Kingdom unless those rights exist already in its law.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> However, the Court of Justice held in December 2011 that Protocol 30 does not operate to exempt the United Kingdom from ensuring compliance with the Charter\'s provisions or from preventing a national court from doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> The extent to which Member States are bound by the Charter was highlighted in a judgment of the European Court of Justice from February 2013 which held that compliance is required where national legislation falls within the scope of European Union law.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other rights are conferred on UK nationals as <a href="/wiki/Citizenship_of_the_European_Union" title="Citizenship of the European Union">citizens of the European Union</a>, notably the right not to be discriminated against on grounds of nationality in an area within the scope of European Union law, the right to move and reside within the EU, the right to vote and stand for election in European and municipal elections, the right to diplomatic protection, the right to petition the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> and the right to apply to the <a href="/wiki/European_Ombudsman" title="European Ombudsman">European Ombudsman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._12-008_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._12-008-248">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> To these rights can be added the "<a href="/wiki/Four_Freedoms_(European_Union)" class="mw-redirect" title="Four Freedoms (European Union)">four freedoms</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/Internal_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal market">European single market</a> which include the right of free movement between Member States, the right to provide services in another Member State, the right to move capital between States and the right to move goods between States without restriction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._5-083_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._5-083-250">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The rights to equal pay and to equal treatment in the workplace and with regard to <a href="/wiki/Social_security" title="Social security">social security</a> are also recognised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._5-063_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELenaertsVan_Nuffel2005para._5-063-251">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights_conferred_by_international_law">Rights conferred by international law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Rights conferred by international law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The United Kingdom is party to a number of international treaties and agreements which guarantee fundamental human rights and freedoms. However, as the UK is a <a href="/wiki/Monism_and_dualism_in_international_law" title="Monism and dualism in international law">dualist</a> state, treaties and agreements ratified by the government
